<p><b>Pinnipeds</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'p' in 'pie'">p</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="'p' in 'pie'">p</span><span title="/ɛ/: 'e' in 'dress'">ɛ</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span><span title="'z' in 'zoom'">z</span></span>/</a></span></span>) (from <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i>pinna</i> "<a href="/wiki/Fin" title="Fin">fin</a>" and <i>pes, pedis</i> "foot"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elias_2007_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Elias_2007-2">[2]</a></sup>), commonly known as <b>seals</b>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[a]</a></sup> are a widely <a href="/wiki/Range_(biology)" title="Range (biology)">distributed</a> and diverse <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">clade</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carnivorous" title="Carnivorous">carnivorous</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fin" title="Fin">fin</a>-footed, <a href="/wiki/List_of_semiaquatic_tetrapods" title="List of semiaquatic tetrapods">semiaquatic</a> <a href="/wiki/Marine_mammal" title="Marine mammal">marine mammals</a>. They comprise the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extant_taxon" title="Extant taxon">extant</a> <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">families</a> <a href="/wiki/Odobenidae" title="Odobenidae">Odobenidae</a> (whose only living member is the <a href="/wiki/Walrus" title="Walrus">walrus</a>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Otariidae" title="Otariidae">Otariidae</a> (the eared seals: <a href="/wiki/Sea_lion" title="Sea lion">sea lions</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fur_seal" title="Fur seal">fur seals</a>), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phocidae" title="Phocidae">Phocidae</a> (the earless seals, or true seals). There are 33 extant species of pinnipeds, and more than 50 extinct species have been described from fossils. While seals were historically thought to have descended from two ancestral lines, <a href="/wiki/Molecular_phylogenetics" title="Molecular phylogenetics">molecular</a> evidence supports them as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monophyletic" title="Monophyletic">monophyletic</a> lineage (descended from one ancestral line). Pinnipeds belong to the order <a href="/wiki/Carnivora" title="Carnivora">Carnivora</a> and their closest living relatives are <a href="/wiki/Bear" title="Bear">bears</a> and <a href="/wiki/Musteloidea" title="Musteloidea">musteloids</a> (<a href="/wiki/Weasel" title="Weasel">weasels</a>, <a href="/wiki/Raccoon" title="Raccoon">raccoons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Skunk" title="Skunk">skunks</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Red_panda" title="Red panda">red pandas</a>), having diverged about 50 million years ago.</p>
<p>Seals range in size from the 1 m (3 ft 3 in) and 45 kg (99 lb) <a href="/wiki/Baikal_seal" title="Baikal seal">Baikal seal</a> to the 5 m (16 ft) and 3,200 kg (7,100 lb) <a href="/wiki/Southern_elephant_seal" title="Southern elephant seal">southern elephant seal</a>, which is also the largest carnivoran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[b]</a></sup> Several species exhibit <a href="/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism" title="Sexual dimorphism">sexual dimorphism</a>. They have streamlined bodies and four limbs that are modified into <a href="/wiki/Flipper_(anatomy)" title="Flipper (anatomy)">flippers</a>. Though not as fast in the water as <a href="/wiki/Dolphin" title="Dolphin">dolphins</a>, seals are more flexible and agile. Otariids use their front limbs primarily to propel themselves through the water, while phocids and walruses use their hind limbs. Otariids and walruses have hind limbs that can be pulled under the body and used as legs on land. By comparison, terrestrial locomotion by phocids is more cumbersome. Otariids have visible external ears, while phocids and walruses lack these. Pinnipeds have well-developed senses—their eyesight and hearing are adapted for both air and water, and they have an advanced <a href="/wiki/Somatosensory_system" title="Somatosensory system">tactile system</a> in their <a href="/wiki/Whiskers" title="Whiskers">whiskers</a> or vibrissae. Some species are well adapted for diving to great depths. They have a layer of fat, or <a href="/wiki/Blubber" title="Blubber">blubber</a>, under the skin to keep warm in the cold water, and, other than the walrus, all species are covered in fur.</p>
<p>Although pinnipeds are widespread, most species prefer the colder waters of the Northern and Southern Hemispheres. They spend most of their lives in the water, but come ashore to mate, give birth, <a href="/wiki/Moulting" title="Moulting">molt</a> or escape from predators, like <a href="/wiki/Shark" title="Shark">sharks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Killer_whale" title="Killer whale">killer whales</a>. They feed largely on fish and <a href="/wiki/Marine_invertebrates" title="Marine invertebrates">marine invertebrates</a>; but a few, like the <a href="/wiki/Leopard_seal" title="Leopard seal">leopard seal</a>, feed on large vertebrates, such as <a href="/wiki/Penguin" title="Penguin">penguins</a> and other seals. Walruses are specialized for feeding on bottom-dwelling <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mollusk" title="Mollusk">mollusks</a>. Male pinnipeds typically mate with more than one female (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polygyny_in_nature" title="Polygyny in nature">polygyny</a>), although the degree of polygyny varies with the species. The males of land-breeding species tend to mate with a greater number of females than those of ice- or water-breeding species. Male pinniped strategies for <a href="/wiki/Reproductive_success" title="Reproductive success">reproductive success</a> vary between defending females, defending <a href="/wiki/Territory_(animal)" title="Territory (animal)">territories</a> that attract females and performing <a href="/wiki/Courtship_display" title="Courtship display">ritual displays</a> or <a href="/wiki/Lek_mating" title="Lek mating">lek mating</a>. Pups are typically born in the spring and summer months and females bear almost all the responsibility for raising them. Mothers of some species <a href="/wiki/Fasting" title="Fasting">fast</a> and nurse their young for a relatively short period of time while others take foraging trips at sea between nursing bouts. Walruses are known to nurse their young while at sea. Seals produce a number of <a href="/wiki/Animal_communication" title="Animal communication">vocalizations</a>, notably the barks of <a href="/wiki/California_sea_lion" title="California sea lion">California sea lions</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Gong" title="Gong">gong</a>-like calls of walruses and the complex songs of <a href="/wiki/Weddell_seal" title="Weddell seal">Weddell seals</a>.</p>
<p>The meat, blubber and fur coats of pinnipeds have traditionally been used by <a href="/wiki/Circumpolar_peoples" title="Circumpolar peoples">indigenous peoples of the Arctic</a>. Seals have been depicted in various cultures worldwide. They are commonly kept in captivity and are even sometimes trained to perform tricks and tasks. Once relentlessly <a href="/wiki/Seal_hunting" title="Seal hunting">hunted</a> by commercial industries for their products, seals and walruses are now protected by international law. The <a href="/wiki/Japanese_sea_lion" title="Japanese sea lion">Japanese sea lion</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_monk_seal" title="Caribbean monk seal">Caribbean monk seal</a> have become extinct in the past century, while the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_monk_seal" title="Mediterranean monk seal">Mediterranean monk seal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hawaiian_monk_seal" title="Hawaiian monk seal">Hawaiian monk seal</a> are ranked <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endangered" title="Endangered">endangered</a> by the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_for_Conservation_of_Nature" title="International Union for Conservation of Nature">International Union for Conservation of Nature</a>. Besides hunting, pinnipeds also face threats from <a href="/wiki/Bycatch" title="Bycatch">accidental trapping</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marine_pollution" title="Marine pollution">marine pollution</a>, and conflicts with local people.</p>
<p></p>

<tr>
<td style="border:0;padding:0 0.2em;border-left:1px solid;border-bottom:1px solid;vertical-align:bottom;text-align:center;"><a href="/wiki/Odobenidae" title="Odobenidae">Odobenidae</a></td>
<td rowspan="2" style="border:0;padding:0;">
<p> <a href="/wiki/Walrus" title="Walrus">walrus</a></p>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="border:0;vertical-align:top;text-align:center;"><br/></td>
</tr>
</table>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="border:0;vertical-align:top;text-align:center;"><br/></td>
</tr>
</table>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="border:0;vertical-align:top;text-align:center;"><br/></td>
</tr>
</table>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="text-align: left; font-size: 88%; border: 0; padding: 0;"><a href="/wiki/Cladogram" title="Cladogram">Cladogram</a> showing relationships among the pinnipeds, combining several phylogenetic analyses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berta_2012_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Berta_2012-5">[3]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The German naturalist <a href="/wiki/Johann_Karl_Wilhelm_Illiger" title="Johann Karl Wilhelm Illiger">Johann Karl Wilhelm Illiger</a> was the first to recognize the pinnipeds as a distinct <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)" title="Taxonomy (biology)">taxonomic</a> unit; in 1811 he gave the name Pinnipedia to both a <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">family</a> and an <a href="/wiki/Order_(biology)" title="Order (biology)">order</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scheffer1958_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Scheffer1958-6">[4]</a></sup> American zoologist <a href="/wiki/Joel_Asaph_Allen" title="Joel Asaph Allen">Joel Asaph Allen</a> reviewed the world's pinnipeds in an 1880 <a href="/wiki/Monograph" title="Monograph">monograph</a>, <i>History of North American pinnipeds, a monograph of the walruses, sea-lions, sea-bears and seals of North America</i>. In this publication, he traced the history of names, gave <a href="/wiki/Identification_key" title="Identification key">keys</a> to families and genera, described North American species and provided synopses of species in other parts of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[5]</a></sup> In 1989, Annalisa Berta and colleagues proposed the unranked <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">clade</a> <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Pinnipedimorpha&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Pinnipedimorpha (page does not exist)">Pinnipedimorpha</a> to contain the fossil genus <i>Enaliarctos</i> and modern seals as a <a href="/wiki/Sister_group" title="Sister group">sister group</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berta_1989_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Berta_1989-8">[6]</a></sup> Pinnipeds belong to the order <a href="/wiki/Carnivora" title="Carnivora">Carnivora</a> and the suborder <a href="/wiki/Caniformia" title="Caniformia">Caniformia</a> (known as dog-like carnivorans).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[7]</a></sup> Pinnipedia was historically considered its own suborder under Carnivora.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[8]</a></sup> Of the three extant families, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Otariidae" title="Otariidae">Otariidae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Odobenidae" title="Odobenidae">Odobenidae</a> are grouped in the superfamily Otarioidea,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[9]</a></sup> while the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phocidae" title="Phocidae">Phocidae</a> belong to the superfamily Phocoidea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>Otariids are also known as eared seals due to the presence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pinna_(anatomy)" title="Pinna (anatomy)">pinnae</a>. These animals rely on their well-developed fore-flippers to propel themselves through the water. They can also turn their hind-flippers forward and "walk" on land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Riedman64_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Riedman64-13">[11]</a></sup> The anterior end of an otariid's <a href="/wiki/Frontal_bone" title="Frontal bone">frontal bones</a> extends between the <a href="/wiki/Nasal_bone" title="Nasal bone">nasal bones</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Supraorbital_foramen" title="Supraorbital foramen">supraorbital foramen</a> is large and flat horizontally. The <a href="/wiki/Supraspinatous_fossa" title="Supraspinatous fossa">supraspinatous fossas</a> are divided by a "secondary spine" and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bronchi" title="Bronchi">bronchi</a> are divided anteriorly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berta_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Berta-14">[12]</a></sup> Otariids consist of two types: <a href="/wiki/Sea_lion" title="Sea lion">sea lions</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fur_seal" title="Fur seal">fur seals</a>. Sea lions are distinguished by their rounder snouts and shorter, rougher <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pelage" title="Pelage">pelage</a>, while fur seals have more pointed snouts, longer fore-flippers and thicker fur coats that include an undercoat and guard hairs. The former also tend to be larger than the latter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[13]</a></sup> Five <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genera" title="Genera">genera</a> and seven species (one now extinct) of sea lion are known to exist, while two genera and nine species of fur seal exist. While sea lions and fur seals have historically been considered separate subfamilies (Otariinae and Arctocephalinae respectively), a 2001 genetic study found that the northern fur seal is more closely related to several sea lion species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[14]</a></sup> This is supported by a 2006 <a href="/wiki/Molecular_phylogenetics" title="Molecular phylogenetics">molecular</a> study that also found that the Australian sea lion and New Zealand sea lion are more closely related to <i><a href="/wiki/Arctocephalus" title="Arctocephalus">Arctocephalus</a></i> than to other sea lions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arnason2006_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Arnason2006-17">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>Odobenidae consists of only one living member: the modern walrus. This animal is easily distinguished from other extant pinnipeds by its larger size (exceeded only by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elephant_seals" title="Elephant seals">elephant seals</a>), nearly hairless skin and long upper <a href="/wiki/Canine_tooth" title="Canine tooth">canines</a>, known as <a href="/wiki/Tusk" title="Tusk">tusks</a>. Like otariids, walruses are capable of turning their hind-flippers forward and can walk on land. When moving in water, the walrus relies on its hind-flippers for locomotion, while its fore-flippers are used for steering. In addition, the walrus lacks external ear flaps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[16]</a></sup> Walruses have <a href="/wiki/Pterygoid_bone" title="Pterygoid bone">pterygoid bones</a> that are broad and thick, frontal bones that are V-shaped at the anterior end and <a href="/wiki/Calcaneus" title="Calcaneus">calcaneuses</a> with pronounced tuberosity in the middle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berta_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Berta-14">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>Phocids are known as true or "earless" seals. These animals lack external ear flaps and are incapable of turning their hind-flippers forward, which makes them more cumbersome on land. In water, true seals swim by moving their hind-flippers and lower body from side to side.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Riedman64_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Riedman64-13">[11]</a></sup> Phocids have <a href="/wiki/Pachyostosis" title="Pachyostosis">thickened</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mastoid_process" title="Mastoid process">mastoids</a>, enlarged <a href="/wiki/Temporal_bone#In_other_animals" title="Temporal bone">entotympanic bones</a>, everted <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pelvic_bone" title="Pelvic bone">pelvic bones</a> and massive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ankle_bone" title="Ankle bone">ankle bones</a>. They also lack supraorbital processes on the frontal and have underdeveloped calcaneal tubers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berta_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-Berta-14">[12]</a></sup> A 2006 molecular study supports the division of phocids into two monophyletic subfamilies: Monachinae, which consists of <i>Mirounga</i>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monachini" title="Monachini">Monachini</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lobodontini" title="Lobodontini">Lobodontini</a>; and Phocinae, which includes <i><a href="/wiki/Pusa" title="Pusa">Pusa</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Phoca" title="Phoca">Phoca</a></i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Halichoerus" title="Halichoerus">Halichoerus</a></i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Histriophoca" title="Histriophoca">Histriophoca</a></i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pagophilus" title="Pagophilus">Pagophilus</a></i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Erignathus" title="Erignathus">Erignathus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Hooded_seal" title="Hooded seal">Cystophora</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arnason2006_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Arnason2006-17">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a 2012 review of pinniped taxonomy, Berta and Morgan Churchill suggested that, based on <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">morphological</a> and genetic criteria, there are 33 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extant_taxon" title="Extant taxon">extant</a> species and 29 subspecies of pinnipeds, although five of the latter lack sufficient support to be conclusively considered subspecies. They recommend that the genus <i>Arctocephalus</i> be limited to <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arctocephalus_pusillus" title="Arctocephalus pusillus">Arctocephalus pusillus</a></i>, and they resurrected the name <i><a href="/wiki/Arctophoca" title="Arctophoca">Arctophoca</a></i> for several species and subspecies formerly placed in <i>Arctocephalus</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berta_2012_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Berta_2012-5">[3]</a></sup> More than 50 fossil species have been described.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[17]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolutionary_history">Evolutionary history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pinniped&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Evolutionary history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Puijila_BW.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="405" data-file-width="800" height="111" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Puijila_BW.jpg/220px-Puijila_BW.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Puijila_BW.jpg/330px-Puijila_BW.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Puijila_BW.jpg/440px-Puijila_BW.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Puijila_BW.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Restoration of <i>Puijila darwini</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>One popular hypothesis suggested that pinnipeds are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polyphyletic" title="Polyphyletic">diphyletic</a> (descended from two ancestral lines), with walruses and otariids sharing a <a href="/wiki/Most_recent_common_ancestor" title="Most recent common ancestor">recent common ancestor</a> with <a href="/wiki/Bear" title="Bear">bears</a> and phocids sharing one with <a href="/wiki/Musteloidea" title="Musteloidea">Musteloidea</a>. However, morphological and molecular evidence support a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monophyletic" title="Monophyletic">monophyletic</a> origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berta_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-Berta-14">[12]</a></sup> Nevertheless, there is some dispute as to whether pinnipeds are more closely related to bears or musteloids, as some studies support the former theory<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Higdon_2007_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Higdon_2007-22">[20]</a></sup> and others the latter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arnason2006_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-Arnason2006-17">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[22]</a></sup> Pinnipeds <a href="/wiki/Cladogenesis" title="Cladogenesis">split</a> from other caniforms 50 million years ago (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mya_(unit)" title="Mya (unit)">mya</a>) during the <a href="/wiki/Eocene" title="Eocene">Eocene</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leptin_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-leptin-25">[23]</a></sup> Their evolutionary link to terrestrial mammals was unknown until the 2007 discovery of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puijila_darwini" title="Puijila darwini">Puijila darwini</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/Early_Miocene" title="Early Miocene">early Miocene</a> deposits in <a href="/wiki/Nunavut" title="Nunavut">Nunavut</a>, Canada. Like a modern <a href="/wiki/Otter" title="Otter">otter</a>, <i>Puijila</i> had a long tail, short limbs and webbed feet instead of flippers. However, its limbs and shoulders were more robust and <i>Puijila</i> likely had been a quadrupedal swimmer–retaining a form of aquatic locomotion that give rise to the major swimming types employed by modern pinnipeds. The researchers who found <i>Puijila</i> placed it in a clade with <i><a href="/wiki/Potamotherium" title="Potamotherium">Potamotherium</a></i> (traditionally considered a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mustelid" title="Mustelid">mustelid</a>) and <i><a href="/wiki/Enaliarctos" title="Enaliarctos">Enaliarctos</a></i>. Of the three, <i>Puijila</i> was the least specialized for aquatic life. The discovery of <i>Puijila</i> in a lake deposit suggests that pinniped evolution went through a freshwater transitional phase.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dawson2009_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dawson2009-26">[24]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Enaliarctos_mealsi.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1329" data-file-width="2086" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Enaliarctos_mealsi.JPG/220px-Enaliarctos_mealsi.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Enaliarctos_mealsi.JPG/330px-Enaliarctos_mealsi.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Enaliarctos_mealsi.JPG/440px-Enaliarctos_mealsi.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Enaliarctos_mealsi.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Fossil of <i>Enaliarctos</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>Enaliarctos</i>, a fossil species of late <a href="/wiki/Oligocene" title="Oligocene">Oligocene</a>/early Miocene (24–22 mya) <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>, closely resembled modern pinnipeds; it was adapted to an aquatic life with a flexible spine, and limbs modified into flippers. Its teeth were adapted for <a href="/wiki/Shearing_(physics)" title="Shearing (physics)">shearing</a> (like terrestrial carnivorans), and it may have stayed near shore more often than its extant relatives. <i>Enaliarctos</i> was capable of swimming with both the fore-flippers and hind-flippers, but it may have been more specialized as a fore-flipper swimmer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berta_14-4"><a href="#cite_note-Berta-14">[12]</a></sup> One species, <i>Enaliarctos emlongi</i>, exhibited notable <a href="/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism" title="Sexual dimorphism">sexual dimorphism</a>, suggesting that this physical characteristic may have been an important driver of pinniped evolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cullenetal2014_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cullenetal2014-27">[25]</a></sup> A closer relative of extant pinnipeds was <i><a href="/wiki/Pteronarctos" title="Pteronarctos">Pteronarctos</a></i>, which lived in <a href="/wiki/Oregon" title="Oregon">Oregon</a> 19–15 mya. As in modern seals, <i>Pteroarctos</i> had an <a href="/wiki/Orbit_(anatomy)" title="Orbit (anatomy)">orbital wall</a> that was not limited by certain facial bones (like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jugal" title="Jugal">jugal</a> or <a href="/wiki/Lacrimal_bone" title="Lacrimal bone">lacrimal bone</a>), but was mostly shaped by the <a href="/wiki/Maxilla" title="Maxilla">maxilla</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berta_14-5"><a href="#cite_note-Berta-14">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Lineage_(evolution)" title="Lineage (evolution)">lineages</a> of Otariidae and Odobenidae split almost 28 mya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arnason2006_17-3"><a href="#cite_note-Arnason2006-17">[15]</a></sup> Otariids originated in the North Pacific. The earliest fossil <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Pithanotaria&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Pithanotaria (page does not exist)">Pithanotaria</a></i>, found in California, is dated to 11 mya. The <i>Callorhinus</i> lineage split earlier at 16 mya. <i><a href="/wiki/Zalophus" title="Zalophus">Zalophus</a></i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eumetopias" title="Eumetopias">Eumetopias</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Otaria" title="Otaria">Otaria</a></i> diverged next, with the latter colonizing the coast of South America. Most of the other otariids diversified in the Southern Hemisphere. The earliest fossils of Odobenidae—<i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Prototaria&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Prototaria (page does not exist)">Prototaria</a></i> of Japan and <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Proneotherium&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Proneotherium (page does not exist)">Proneotherium</a></i> of Oregon—date to 18–16 mya. These primitive walruses had much shorter canines and lived on a fish diet rather than a specialized mollusk diet like the modern walrus. Odobenids further diversified in the middle and <a href="/wiki/Late_Miocene" title="Late Miocene">late Miocene</a>. Several species had enlarged upper and lower canines. The genera <i><a href="/wiki/Valenictus" title="Valenictus">Valenictus</a></i> and <i>Odobenus</i> developed elongated tusks. The lineage of the modern walrus may have spread from the North Pacific to the Caribbean (via the <a href="/wiki/Central_American_Seaway" title="Central American Seaway">Central American Seaway</a>) 8–5 mya and subsequently made it to the North Atlantic and returned to the North Pacific via the Arctic 1 mya. Alternatively, this lineage may have spread from the North Pacific to the Arctic and subsequently the North Atlantic during the <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berta_14-6"><a href="#cite_note-Berta-14">[12]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Desmatophoca_oregonensis.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="730" data-file-width="1198" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Desmatophoca_oregonensis.jpg/220px-Desmatophoca_oregonensis.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Desmatophoca_oregonensis.jpg/330px-Desmatophoca_oregonensis.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Desmatophoca_oregonensis.jpg/440px-Desmatophoca_oregonensis.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Desmatophoca_oregonensis.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Fossil skull cast of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Desmatophoca_oregonensis" title="Desmatophoca oregonensis">Desmatophoca oregonensis</a></i> from the extinct Desmatophocidae</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The ancestors of the Otarioidea and Phocoidea diverged 33 mya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leptin_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-leptin-25">[23]</a></sup> The Phocidae are likely to have descended from the extinct family <a href="/wiki/Desmatophocidae" title="Desmatophocidae">Desmatophocidae</a> in the North Atlantic. Desmatophocids lived 23–10 mya and had elongated skulls, fairly large eyes, <a href="/wiki/Zygomatic_bone" title="Zygomatic bone">cheekbones</a> connected by a <a href="/wiki/Mortise_and_tenon" title="Mortise and tenon">mortised</a> structure and rounded cheek teeth. They also were sexually dimorphic and may have been capable of propelling themselves with both the foreflippers and hindflippers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berta_14-7"><a href="#cite_note-Berta-14">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>Phocids are known to have existed for at least 15 million years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berta_14-8"><a href="#cite_note-Berta-14">[12]</a></sup> and molecular evidence supports a divergence of the Monachinae and Phocinae lineages 22 mya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arnason2006_17-4"><a href="#cite_note-Arnason2006-17">[15]</a></sup> The fossil monachine <i><a href="/wiki/Monotherium" title="Monotherium">Monotherium</a></i> and phocine <i><a href="/wiki/Leptophoca" title="Leptophoca">Leptophoca</a></i> were found in southeastern North America. The deep split between the lineages of <i>Erignathus</i> and <i>Cystophora</i> 17 mya suggests that the phocines migrated eastward and northward from the North Atlantic. The genera <i>Phoca</i> and <i>Pusa</i> could have arisen when a phocine lineage traveled from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paratethys_Sea" title="Paratethys Sea">Paratethys Sea</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Basin" title="Arctic Basin">Arctic Basin</a> and subsequently went eastward. The ancestor of the Baikal seal migrated into <a href="/wiki/Lake_Baikal" title="Lake Baikal">Lake Baikal</a> from the Arctic (via the Siberian ice sheet) and became isolated there. The Caspian seal's ancestor became isolated as the Paratethys shrank, leaving the animal in a small remnant sea, the <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian Sea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berta_14-9"><a href="#cite_note-Berta-14">[12]</a></sup> The monochines diversified southward. <i>Monachus</i> emerged in the Mediterranean and migrated to the Caribbean and then the central North Pacific.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fyler2005_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fyler2005-28">[26]</a></sup> The two extant elephant seal species diverged close to 4 mya after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Panamanian_isthmus" title="Panamanian isthmus">Panamanian isthmus</a> was formed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arnason2006_17-5"><a href="#cite_note-Arnason2006-17">[15]</a></sup> The lobodontine lineage emerged around 9 mya and colonized the southern ocean in response to <a href="/wiki/Glacial_period" title="Glacial period">glaciation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fyler2005_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fyler2005-28">[26]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Anatomy_and_physiology">Anatomy and physiology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pinniped&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Anatomy and physiology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tmulti tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zalophus_californianus_01.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="3168" data-file-width="4423" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Zalophus_californianus_01.JPG/200px-Zalophus_californianus_01.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Zalophus_californianus_01.JPG/300px-Zalophus_californianus_01.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Zalophus_californianus_01.JPG/400px-Zalophus_californianus_01.JPG 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mirounga_leonina_01.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="3144" data-file-width="4888" height="129" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Mirounga_leonina_01.jpg/200px-Mirounga_leonina_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Mirounga_leonina_01.jpg/300px-Mirounga_leonina_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Mirounga_leonina_01.jpg/400px-Mirounga_leonina_01.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Skeleton of <a href="/wiki/California_sea_lion" title="California sea lion">California sea lion</a> (above) and <a href="/wiki/Southern_elephant_seal" title="Southern elephant seal">southern elephant seal</a></div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Pinnipeds have streamlined, spindle-shaped bodies with reduced or non-existent external ear flaps, rounded heads, flexible necks, limbs modified into flippers, and small tails.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-overview_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-overview-29">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[28]</a></sup> Pinniped skulls have large eye orbits, short <a href="/wiki/Snout" title="Snout">snouts</a> and a constricted <a href="/wiki/Interorbital_region" title="Interorbital region">interorbital region</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[29]</a></sup> They are unique among carnivorans in that their orbital walls are significantly shaped by the maxilla and are not limited by certain facial bones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berta_14-10"><a href="#cite_note-Berta-14">[12]</a></sup> Compared to other carnivorans, their teeth tend to be fewer in number (especially <a href="/wiki/Incisor" title="Incisor">incisors</a> and back <a href="/wiki/Molar_(tooth)" title="Molar (tooth)">molars</a>), are pointed and cone-shaped, and lack <a href="/wiki/Carnassial" title="Carnassial">carnassials</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[30]</a></sup> The walrus has unique upper canines that are elongated into tusks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[31]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Mammary_gland" title="Mammary gland">mammary glands</a> and genitals of pinnipeds can retract into the body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-overview_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-overview-29">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>Pinnipeds range in size from the 1 m (3 ft 3 in) and 45 kg (99 lb) Baikal seal to the 5 m (16 ft) and 3,200 kg (7,100 lb) southern elephant seal. Overall, they tend to be larger than other carnivorans; the southern elephant seal is the largest carnivoran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-overview_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-overview-29">[27]</a></sup> Several species have male-biased sexual dimorphism that correlates with the degree of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polygyny_in_nature" title="Polygyny in nature">polygyny</a> in a species: highly polygynous species like elephant seals are extremely sexually dimorphic, while less polygynous species have males and females that are closer in size. In lobodontine seals, females are slightly larger than males. Males of sexually dimorphic species also tend to have <a href="/wiki/Secondary_sex_characteristic" title="Secondary sex characteristic">secondary sex characteristics</a>, such as the prominent <a href="/wiki/Proboscis" title="Proboscis">proboscis</a> of elephant seals, the inflatable red nasal membrane of hooded seals and the thick necks and manes of otariids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dimorphism_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-dimorphism-34">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berta73_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Berta73-35">[33]</a></sup> Despite a correlation between size dimorphism and the degree of polygyny, some evidence suggests that size differences between the sexes originated due to ecological differences and prior to the development of polygyny.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GonzalezSuarezCassini2014_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-GonzalezSuarezCassini2014-36">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krugeretal2014_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Krugeretal2014-37">[35]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Southern_Sea_Lions.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1956" data-file-width="1956" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Southern_Sea_Lions.jpg/220px-Southern_Sea_Lions.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Southern_Sea_Lions.jpg/330px-Southern_Sea_Lions.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Southern_Sea_Lions.jpg/440px-Southern_Sea_Lions.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Southern_Sea_Lions.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Male and female South American sea lions, showing <a href="/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism" title="Sexual dimorphism">sexual dimorphism</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Almost all pinnipeds have fur coats, the exception being the walrus, which is only sparsely covered. Even some fully furred species (particularly sea lions) are less haired than most land mammals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Riedman3_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Riedman3-38">[36]</a></sup> In species that live on ice, young pups have thicker coats than adults. The individual hairs on the coat, known collectively as <a href="/wiki/Lanugo" title="Lanugo">lanugo</a>, can trap heat from sunlight and keep the pup warm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Riedman16_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Riedman16-39">[37]</a></sup> Pinnipeds are typically <a href="/wiki/Countershading" title="Countershading">countershaded</a>, and are darker colored <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dorsum_(anatomy)" title="Dorsum (anatomy)">dorsally</a> and lighter colored <a href="/wiki/Abdomen" title="Abdomen">ventrally</a>, which serves to eliminate shadows caused by light shining over the ocean water. The pure white fur of harp seal pups conceals them in their Arctic environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[38]</a></sup> Some species, such as ribbon seals, ringed seals and leopard seals, have patterns of contrasting light and dark coloration. All fully furred species <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molt" title="Molt">molt</a>; phocids molt once a year, while otariids gradually molt all year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[39]</a></sup> Seals have a layer of <a href="/wiki/Panniculus_adiposus" title="Panniculus adiposus">subcutaneous fat</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Blubber" title="Blubber">blubber</a> that is particularly thick in phocids and walruses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-overview_29-3"><a href="#cite_note-overview-29">[27]</a></sup> Blubber serves both to keep the animals warm and to provide energy and nourishment when they are <a href="/wiki/Fasting" title="Fasting">fasting</a>. It can constitute as much as 50% of a pinniped's body weight. Pups are born with only a thin layer of blubber, but some species compensate for this with thick lanugos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Riedman16_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-Riedman16-39">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>Pinnipeds have a simple stomach that is similar in structure to terrestrial carnivores. Most species have neither a <a href="/wiki/Cecum" title="Cecum">cecum</a> nor a clear demarcation between the <a href="/wiki/Small_intestine" title="Small intestine">small</a> and <a href="/wiki/Large_intestine" title="Large intestine">large intestines</a>; the large intestine is comparatively short and only slightly wider than the latter. Small intestine lengths range from 8 (California sea lion) to 25 times (elephant seal) the body length. The length of the intestine may be an adaptation to frequent deep diving, as the increased volume of the digestive tract serves as an extended storage compartment for partially digested food during submersion. Pinnipeds do not have an <a href="/wiki/Appendix_(anatomy)" title="Appendix (anatomy)">appendix</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[40]</a></sup> As in most marine mammals, the <a href="/wiki/Reniculate_kidney" title="Reniculate kidney">kidneys</a> are divided into small lobes and can effectively absorb water and filter out excess salt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Riedman31_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Riedman31-43">[41]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Locomotion">Locomotion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pinniped&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Locomotion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pinniped_underwater.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1918" data-file-width="2374" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Pinniped_underwater.jpg/200px-Pinniped_underwater.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Pinniped_underwater.jpg/300px-Pinniped_underwater.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Pinniped_underwater.jpg/400px-Pinniped_underwater.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bigmalesealion.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Bigmalesealion.jpg/200px-Bigmalesealion.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Bigmalesealion.jpg/300px-Bigmalesealion.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Bigmalesealion.jpg/400px-Bigmalesealion.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Harbor_seal" title="Harbor seal">Harbor seal</a> (above) and California sea lion swimming. The former swims with its hind-flippers, the latter with its fore-flippers.</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Pinnipeds have two pairs of flippers on the front and back, the fore-flippers and hind-flippers. The elbows and ankles are enclosed within the body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berta63_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Berta63-44">[42]</a></sup> Pinnipeds tend to be slower swimmers than <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cetacean" title="Cetacean">cetaceans</a>, typically cruising at 5–15 <a href="/wiki/Knot_(unit)" title="Knot (unit)">kn</a> (9–28 km/h; 6–17 mph) compared to around 20 kn (37 km/h; 23 mph) for several species of <a href="/wiki/Dolphin" title="Dolphin">dolphin</a>. Seals are more agile and flexible,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Riedman3_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-Riedman3-38">[36]</a></sup> and some otariids, such as the California sea lion, are capable of bending their necks backwards far enough to reach their hind-flippers, allowing them to make dorsal turns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fish_2003_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fish_2003-45">[43]</a></sup> Pinnipeds have several adaptions for reducing <a href="/wiki/Drag_(physics)" title="Drag (physics)">drag</a>. In addition to their streamlined bodies, they have smooth networks of <a href="/wiki/Muscle_fascicle" title="Muscle fascicle">muscle bundles</a> in their skin that may increase <a href="/wiki/Laminar_flow" title="Laminar flow">laminar flow</a> and make it easier for them to slip through water. They also lack <a href="/wiki/Arrector_pili_muscle" title="Arrector pili muscle">arrector pili</a>, so their fur can be streamlined as they swim.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Riedman3_38-2"><a href="#cite_note-Riedman3-38">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>When swimming, otariids rely on their fore-flippers for locomotion in a wing-like manner similar to <a href="/wiki/Penguin" title="Penguin">penguins</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_turtles" title="Sea turtles">sea turtles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fish1996_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fish1996-46">[44]</a></sup> Fore-flipper movement is not continuous, and the animal glides between each stroke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fish_2003_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fish_2003-45">[43]</a></sup> Compared to terrestrial carnivorans, the fore-limbs of otariids are reduced in length, which gives the locomotor muscles at the shoulder and elbow joints greater mechanical advantage;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berta63_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Berta63-44">[42]</a></sup> the hind-flippers serve as stabilizers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Riedman3_38-3"><a href="#cite_note-Riedman3-38">[36]</a></sup> Phocids and walruses swim by moving their hind-flippers and lower body from side to side,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fish1996_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fish1996-46">[44]</a></sup> while their fore-flippers are mainly used for steering.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berta63_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-Berta63-44">[42]</a></sup> Some species <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Whale_surfacing_behaviour#Breaching.2C_lunging.2C_and_porpoising" title="Whale surfacing behaviour">leap</a> out of the water, which may allow then to travel faster. In addition, sea lions are known to "ride" waves, which probably helps them decrease their energy usage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Riedman3_38-4"><a href="#cite_note-Riedman3-38">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>Pinnipeds can move around on land, though not as well as terrestrial animals. Otariids and walruses are capable of turning their hind-flippers forward and under the body so they can "walk" on all fours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Riedman11_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Riedman11-47">[45]</a></sup> The fore-flippers move in a <a href="/wiki/Transverse_plane" title="Transverse plane">transverse</a>, rather than a <a href="/wiki/Sagittal_plane" title="Sagittal plane">sagittal</a> fashion. Otariids rely on the movements of their heads and necks more than their hind-flippers during terrestrial locomotion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[46]</a></sup> By swinging their heads and necks, otariids create momentum while they are moving. Sea lions have been recorded climbing up flights of stairs. Phocids are less agile on land. They cannot pull their hind-flippers forward, and move on land by lunging, bouncing and wiggling while their fore-flippers keep them balanced. Some species use their fore-flippers to pull themselves forward. Terrestrial locomotion is easier for phocids on ice, as they can sled along.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Riedman11_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-Riedman11-47">[45]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Senses">Senses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pinniped&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Senses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Seals_at_Cape_Cross,_Namibia_(3045707919).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3008" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Seals_at_Cape_Cross%2C_Namibia_%283045707919%29.jpg/220px-Seals_at_Cape_Cross%2C_Namibia_%283045707919%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Seals_at_Cape_Cross%2C_Namibia_%283045707919%29.jpg/330px-Seals_at_Cape_Cross%2C_Namibia_%283045707919%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Seals_at_Cape_Cross%2C_Namibia_%283045707919%29.jpg/440px-Seals_at_Cape_Cross%2C_Namibia_%283045707919%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Seals_at_Cape_Cross,_Namibia_(3045707919).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Frontal view of <a href="/wiki/Brown_fur_seal" title="Brown fur seal">brown fur seal</a> head</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The eyes of pinnipeds are relatively large for their size and are positioned near the front of the head. One exception is the walrus, whose smaller eyes are located on the sides of its head.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Riedman43_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Riedman43-49">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berta.2C_p._67_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Berta.2C_p._67-50">[48]</a></sup> This is because it feeds on immobile bottom dwelling mollusks and hence does not need acute vision.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Riedman43_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-Riedman43-49">[47]</a></sup> A seal's eye is adapted for seeing both underwater and in air. The <a href="/wiki/Lens_(anatomy)" title="Lens (anatomy)">lens</a> is mostly spherical, and much of the <a href="/wiki/Retina" title="Retina">retina</a> is <a href="/wiki/Equidistant" title="Equidistant">equidistant</a> from the lens center. The <a href="/wiki/Cornea" title="Cornea">cornea</a> has a flattened center where <a href="/wiki/Refraction" title="Refraction">refraction</a> is nearly equal in both water and air. Pinnipeds also have very muscular and vascularized <a href="/wiki/Iris_(anatomy)" title="Iris (anatomy)">irises</a>. The well-developed <a href="/wiki/Iris_dilator_muscle" title="Iris dilator muscle">dilator muscle</a> gives the animals a great range in <a href="/wiki/Pupillary_response" title="Pupillary response">pupil dilation</a>. When contracted, the pupil is typically pear-shaped, although the bearded seal's is more <a href="/wiki/Diagonal" title="Diagonal">diagonal</a>. In species that live in shallow water, such as harbor seals and California sea lions, dilation varies little, while the deep-diving elephant seals have much greater variation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>On land, pinnipeds are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Myopia" title="Myopia">near-sighted</a> in dim light. This is reduced in bright light, as the retracted pupil reduces the lens and cornea's ability to bend light. They also have a well-developed <i><a href="/wiki/Tapetum_lucidum" title="Tapetum lucidum">tapetum lucidum</a></i>, a reflecting layer that increases sensitivity by reflecting light back through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rod_cells" title="Rod cells">rods</a>. This helps them see in low-light conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Riedman43_49-2"><a href="#cite_note-Riedman43-49">[47]</a></sup> Ice-living seals like the harp seal have corneas that can tolerate high levels of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ultraviolet_radiation" title="Ultraviolet radiation">ultraviolet radiation</a> typical of bright, snowy environments. As such, they do not suffer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Snow_blindness" title="Snow blindness">snow blindness</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[50]</a></sup> Pinnipeds appear to have limited <a href="/wiki/Color_vision" title="Color vision">color vision</a>, as they lack <a href="/wiki/Cone_cell" title="Cone cell">S-cones</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[51]</a></sup> Flexible eye movement has been documented in seals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[52]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Extraocular_muscles" title="Extraocular muscles">extraocular muscles</a> of the walrus are well developed. This and its lack of orbital roof allow it to protrude its eyes and see in both frontal and dorsal directions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[53]</a></sup> Seals release large amounts of <a href="/wiki/Mucus" title="Mucus">mucus</a> to protect their eyes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Riedman3_38-5"><a href="#cite_note-Riedman3-38">[36]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Corneal_epithelium" title="Corneal epithelium">corneal epithelium</a> is <a href="/wiki/Keratin" title="Keratin">keratinized</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sclera" title="Sclera">sclera</a> is thick enough to withstand the pressures of diving. As in many mammals and birds, pinnipeds possess <a href="/wiki/Nictitating_membrane" title="Nictitating membrane">nictitating membranes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>The pinniped ear is adapted for hearing underwater, where it can hear sound frequencies at up to 70,000 <a href="/wiki/Hertz" title="Hertz">Hz</a>. In air, hearing is somewhat reduced in pinnipeds compared to many terrestrial mammals. While they are capable of hearing a wide range of frequencies (e.g. 500 to 32,000 Hz in the <a href="/wiki/Northern_fur_seal" title="Northern fur seal">northern fur seal</a>, compared to 20 to 20,000 Hz in humans), their airborne hearing sensitivity is weaker overall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[55]</a></sup> One study of three species—the harbor seal, California sea lion and northern elephant seal—found that the sea lion was best adapted for airborne hearing, the harbor seal was equally capable of hearing in air and water, and the elephant seal was better adapted for underwater hearing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[56]</a></sup> Although pinnipeds have a fairly good sense of smell on land,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[57]</a></sup> it is useless underwater as their nostrils are closed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[58]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Walrus_-_Kamogawa_Seaworld_-_1.jpg"><img alt='Photo of walrus head in profile showing one eye, nose, tusks, and "mustache"' class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2912" data-file-width="4368" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Walrus_-_Kamogawa_Seaworld_-_1.jpg/220px-Walrus_-_Kamogawa_Seaworld_-_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Walrus_-_Kamogawa_Seaworld_-_1.jpg/330px-Walrus_-_Kamogawa_Seaworld_-_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Walrus_-_Kamogawa_Seaworld_-_1.jpg/440px-Walrus_-_Kamogawa_Seaworld_-_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Walrus_-_Kamogawa_Seaworld_-_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vibrissa" title="Vibrissa">Vibrissae</a> of walrus</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Pinnipeds have well-developed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tactition" title="Tactition">tactile</a> senses. Their mystacial <a href="/wiki/Whiskers" title="Whiskers">vibrissae</a> have ten times the <a href="/wiki/Nerve" title="Nerve">innervation</a> of terrestrial mammals, allowing them to effectively detect vibrations in the water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[59]</a></sup> These vibrations are generated, for example, when a fish swims through water. Detecting vibrations is useful when the animals are foraging and may add to or even replace vision, particularly in darkness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-whiskers_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-whiskers-62">[60]</a></sup> Harbor seals have been observed following varying paths of another seal that swam ahead several minutes before, similar to a dog following a scent trail,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dehnhardt_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-dehnhardt-63">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schulte-pelkum_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-schulte-pelkum-64">[62]</a></sup> and even to discriminate the species and the size of the fish responsible for the trail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sealdiscrim_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-sealdiscrim-65">[63]</a></sup> Blind ringed seals have even been observed successfully hunting on their own in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lake_Saimaa" title="Lake Saimaa">Lake Saimaa</a>, likely relying on their vibrissae to gain sensory information and catch prey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blindseals_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-blindseals-66">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>Unlike terrestrial mammals, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rodents" title="Rodents">rodents</a>, pinnipeds do not move their vibrissae over an object when examining it but instead extend their moveable whiskers and keep them in the same position.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-whiskers_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-whiskers-62">[60]</a></sup> By holding their vibrissae steady, pinnipeds are able to maximize their detection ability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-angle_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-angle-67">[65]</a></sup> The vibrissae of phocids are undulated and wavy while otariid and walrus vibrissae are smooth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vibshape_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-vibshape-68">[66]</a></sup> Research is ongoing to determine the function, if any, of these shapes on detection ability. The vibrissa's angle relative to the flow, not the shape, however, seems to be the most important factor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-angle_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-angle-67">[65]</a></sup> The vibrissae of some otariids grow quite long—those of the Antarctic fur seal can reach 41 cm (16 in).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[67]</a></sup> Walruses have the most vibrissae, at 600–700 individual hairs. These are important for detecting their prey on the muddy sea floor. In addition to foraging, vibrissae may also play a role in navigation; spotted seals appear to use them to detect breathing holes in the ice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Riedman42_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Riedman42-70">[68]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diving_adaptations">Diving adaptations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pinniped&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Diving adaptations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Diving_weddell_seals.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Diving_weddell_seals.jpg/220px-Diving_weddell_seals.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Diving_weddell_seals.jpg/330px-Diving_weddell_seals.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Diving_weddell_seals.jpg/440px-Diving_weddell_seals.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Diving_weddell_seals.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Diving <a href="/wiki/Weddell_seal" title="Weddell seal">Weddell seals</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Before diving, pinnipeds typically exhale to empty their lungs of half the air<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Riedman25_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Riedman25-71">[69]</a></sup> and then close their nostrils and throat cartilages to protect the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vertebrate_trachea" title="Vertebrate trachea">trachea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[70]</a></sup> Their unique lungs have airways that are highly reinforced with <a href="/wiki/Cartilage" title="Cartilage">cartilaginous rings</a> and <a href="/wiki/Smooth_muscle_tissue" title="Smooth muscle tissue">smooth muscle</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveoli" title="Alveoli">alveoli</a> that completely deflate during deeper dives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[72]</a></sup> While terrestrial mammals are generally unable to empty their lungs,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-miller2006b_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-miller2006b-75">[73]</a></sup> pinnipeds can reinflate their lungs even after complete respiratory collapse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[74]</a></sup> The middle ear contains <a href="/wiki/Sinus_(anatomy)" title="Sinus (anatomy)">sinuses</a> that probably fill with blood during dives, preventing <a href="/wiki/Ear_clearing" title="Ear clearing">middle ear squeeze</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Costa_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Costa-77">[75]</a></sup> The heart of a seal is moderately flattened to allow the lungs to deflate. The trachea is flexible enough to collapse under pressure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Riedman25_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-Riedman25-71">[69]</a></sup> During deep dives, any remaining air in their bodies is stored in the <a href="/wiki/Bronchiole" title="Bronchiole">bronchioles</a> and trachea, which prevents them from experiencing <a href="/wiki/Decompression_sickness" title="Decompression sickness">decompression sickness</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxygen_toxicity" title="Oxygen toxicity">oxygen toxicity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_narcosis" title="Nitrogen narcosis">nitrogen narcosis</a>. In addition, seals can tolerate large amounts of <a href="/wiki/Lactic_acid" title="Lactic acid">lactic acid</a>, which reduces skeletal muscle fatigue during intense physical activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Costa_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-Costa-77">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>The main adaptations of the pinniped <a href="/wiki/Circulatory_system" title="Circulatory system">circulatory system</a> for diving are the enlargement and increased complexity of veins to increase their capacity. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retia_mirabilia" title="Retia mirabilia">Retia mirabilia</a> form blocks of tissue on the inner wall of the <a href="/wiki/Thoracic_cavity" title="Thoracic cavity">thoracic cavity</a> and the body periphery. These tissue masses, which contain extensive contorted spirals of arteries and thin-walled veins, act as blood reservoirs that increase oxygen stores for use during diving.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berta_et_al._p.241_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Berta_et_al._p.241-78">[76]</a></sup> As with other diving mammals, pinnipeds have high amounts of <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">hemoglobin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Myoglobin" title="Myoglobin">myoglobin</a> stored in their blood and muscles. This allows them to stay submerged for long periods of time while still having enough oxygen. Deep-diving species such as elephant seals have <a href="/wiki/Blood_volume" title="Blood volume">blood volumes</a> that make up to 20% of their body weight. When diving, they reduce their heart rate and maintain blood flow only to the heart, brain and lungs. To keep their <a href="/wiki/Blood_pressure" title="Blood pressure">blood pressure</a> stable, phocids have an elastic <a href="/wiki/Aorta" title="Aorta">aorta</a> that dissipates some energy of each heartbeat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Costa_77-2"><a href="#cite_note-Costa-77">[75]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Thermoregulation">Thermoregulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pinniped&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Thermoregulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:N_elephant_seal_resting.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2272" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/N_elephant_seal_resting.JPG/220px-N_elephant_seal_resting.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/N_elephant_seal_resting.JPG/330px-N_elephant_seal_resting.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/N_elephant_seal_resting.JPG/440px-N_elephant_seal_resting.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:N_elephant_seal_resting.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Northern elephant seal resting in water</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Pinnipeds conserve heat with their large and compact body size, insulating blubber and fur, and high metabolism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[77]</a></sup> In addition, the blood vessels in their flippers are adapted for <a href="/wiki/Countercurrent_exchange" title="Countercurrent exchange">countercurrent exchange</a>. Veins containing cool blood from the body extremities surround arteries, which contain warm blood received from the core of the body. Heat from the arterial blood is transferred to the blood vessels, which then recirculate blood back to the core.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[78]</a></sup> The same adaptations that conserve heat while in water tend to inhibit heat loss when out of water. To counteract overheating, many species cool off by flipping sand onto their backs, adding a layer of cool, damp sand that enhances heat loss. The northern fur seal <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/pant" title="wikt:pant">pants</a> to help stay cool, while monk seals often dig holes in the sand to expose cooler layers to rest in.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[79]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sleep">Sleep</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pinniped&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Sleep">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Pinnipeds spend many months at a time at sea, so they must sleep in the water. Scientists have recorded them sleeping for minutes at a time while slowly drifting downward in a belly-up orientation. Like other marine mammals, seals sleep in water with <a href="/wiki/Unihemispheric_slow-wave_sleep" title="Unihemispheric slow-wave sleep">half of their brain awake</a> so that they can detect and escape from predators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sleep_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-sleep-82">[80]</a></sup> When they are asleep on land, both sides of their brain go into sleep mode.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[81]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution_and_habitat">Distribution and habitat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pinniped&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Distribution and habitat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Noaa-walrus22.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1068" data-file-width="1424" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Noaa-walrus22.jpg/220px-Noaa-walrus22.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Noaa-walrus22.jpg/330px-Noaa-walrus22.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Noaa-walrus22.jpg/440px-Noaa-walrus22.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Noaa-walrus22.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Walrus on ice off Alaska. This species has a discontinuous distribution around the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Circle" title="Arctic Circle">Arctic Circle</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Living pinnipeds mainly inhabit polar and subpolar regions, particularly the North Atlantic, the North Pacific and the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ocean" title="Southern Ocean">Southern Ocean</a>. They are entirely absent from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indomalaya_ecozone" title="Indomalaya ecozone">Indo-Malayan</a> waters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacDonald_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald-84">[82]</a></sup> Monk seals and some otariids live in tropical and subtropical waters. Seals usually require cool, nutrient-rich waters with temperatures lower than 20 °C (68 °F). Even those that live in warm or tropical climates live in areas that become cold and nutrient rich due to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Current_(ocean)" title="Current (ocean)">current</a> patterns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacDonald_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald-84">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[83]</a></sup> Only monk seals live in waters that are not typically cool or rich in nutrients.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacDonald_84-2"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald-84">[82]</a></sup> The Caspian seal and Baikal seal are found in large landlocked bodies of water (the Caspian Sea and Lake Baikal respectively).</p>
<p>As a whole, pinnipeds can be found in a variety of aquatic habitats, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coastal" title="Coastal">coastal</a> water, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Open_ocean" title="Open ocean">open ocean</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brackish_water" title="Brackish water">brackish water</a> and even freshwater <a href="/wiki/Lake" title="Lake">lakes</a> and <a href="/wiki/River" title="River">rivers</a>. Most species inhabit coastal areas, though some travel offshore and feed in deep waters off <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oceanic_islands" title="Oceanic islands">oceanic islands</a>. The Baikal seal is the only freshwater species, though some ringed seals live in freshwater lakes in Russia close to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baltic_sea" title="Baltic sea">Baltic sea</a>. In addition, harbor seals may visit <a href="/wiki/Estuary" title="Estuary">estuaries</a>, lakes and rivers and sometimes stay as long as a year. Other species known to enter freshwater include California sea lions and South American sea lions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[84]</a></sup> Pinnipeds also use a number of terrestrial habitats and <a href="/wiki/Substrate_(biology)" title="Substrate (biology)">substrates</a>, both continental and island. In temperate and tropical areas, they <a href="/wiki/Hauling-out" title="Hauling-out">haul-out</a> on to sandy and <a href="/wiki/Pebble" title="Pebble">pebble</a> beaches, <a href="/wiki/Rocky_shore" title="Rocky shore">rocky shores</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shoal" title="Shoal">shoals</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mud_flat" title="Mud flat">mud flats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tide_pool" title="Tide pool">tide pools</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Sea_cave" title="Sea cave">sea caves</a>. Some species also rest on man-made structures, like <a href="/wiki/Pier" title="Pier">piers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jetty" title="Jetty">jetties</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buoy" title="Buoy">buoys</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oil_platform" title="Oil platform">oil platforms</a>. Pinnipeds may move further inland and rest in sand dunes or vegetation, and may even climb cliffs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[85]</a></sup> Polar-living species haul-out on to both <a href="/wiki/Fast_ice" title="Fast ice">fast ice</a> and <a href="/wiki/Drift_ice" title="Drift ice">drift ice</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[86]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Behavior_and_life_history">Behavior and life history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pinniped&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Behavior and life history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Phoca_vitulina_(Habor_Seal),_Point_Lobos,_CA,_US_-_Diliff.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2482" data-file-width="3722" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Phoca_vitulina_%28Habor_Seal%29%2C_Point_Lobos%2C_CA%2C_US_-_Diliff.jpg/220px-Phoca_vitulina_%28Habor_Seal%29%2C_Point_Lobos%2C_CA%2C_US_-_Diliff.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Phoca_vitulina_%28Habor_Seal%29%2C_Point_Lobos%2C_CA%2C_US_-_Diliff.jpg/330px-Phoca_vitulina_%28Habor_Seal%29%2C_Point_Lobos%2C_CA%2C_US_-_Diliff.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Phoca_vitulina_%28Habor_Seal%29%2C_Point_Lobos%2C_CA%2C_US_-_Diliff.jpg/440px-Phoca_vitulina_%28Habor_Seal%29%2C_Point_Lobos%2C_CA%2C_US_-_Diliff.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Phoca_vitulina_(Habor_Seal),_Point_Lobos,_CA,_US_-_Diliff.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Harbor seal hauled out on rock</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Pinnipeds have an <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/amphibious" title="wikt:amphibious">amphibious</a> lifestyle; they spend most of their lives in the water, but haul-out to mate, raise young, molt, rest, <a href="/wiki/Thermoregulation" title="Thermoregulation">thermoregulate</a> or escape from aquatic predators. Several species are known to <a href="/wiki/Animal_migration" title="Animal migration">migrate</a> vast distances, particularly in response to extreme environmental changes, like <a href="/wiki/El_Ni%C3%B1o" title="El Niño">El Niño</a> or changes in ice cover. <a href="/wiki/Elephant_seal" title="Elephant seal">Elephant seals</a> stay at sea 8–10 months a year and migrate between breeding and molting sites. The northern elephant seal has one of the longest recorded migration distances for a mammal, at 18,000–21,000 km (11,000–13,000 mi). Phocids tend to migrate more than otariids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[87]</a></sup> Traveling seals may use various features of their environment to reach their destination including <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field" title="Earth's magnetic field">geomagnetic fields</a>, water and wind currents, the position of the sun and moon and the taste and temperature of the water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>Pinnipeds may dive during foraging or to avoid predators. When foraging, Weddell seals typically dive for less than 15 minutes to depths of around 400 m (1,300 ft) but can dive for as long as 73 minutes and to depths of up to 600 m (2,000 ft). Northern elephant seals commonly dive 350–650 m (1,150–2,130 ft) for as long as 20 minutes. They can also dive 1,259–4,100 m (4,131–13,451 ft) and for as long as 62 minutes. The dives of otariids tend to be shorter and less deep. They typically last 5–7 minutes with average depths to 30–45 m (98–148 ft). However, the New Zealand sea lion has been recorded diving to a maximum of 460 m (1,510 ft) and a duration of 12 minutes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[89]</a></sup> Walruses do not often dive very deep, as they feed in shallow water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>Pinnipeds have lifespans averaging 25–30 years. Females usually live longer, as males tend to fight and often die before reaching maturity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[91]</a></sup> The longest recorded lifespans include 43 years for a wild female ringed seal and 46 years for a wild female grey seal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[92]</a></sup> The age at which a pinniped sexually matures can vary from 2–12 years depending on the species. Females typically mature earlier than males.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[93]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foraging_and_predation">Foraging and predation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pinniped&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Foraging and predation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:272px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sea_lion_and_Sturgeon.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1296" data-file-width="2304" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Sea_lion_and_Sturgeon.jpg/270px-Sea_lion_and_Sturgeon.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Sea_lion_and_Sturgeon.jpg/405px-Sea_lion_and_Sturgeon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Sea_lion_and_Sturgeon.jpg/540px-Sea_lion_and_Sturgeon.jpg 2x" width="270"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sea_lion_and_Sturgeon.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Steller sea lion with <a href="/wiki/White_sturgeon" title="White sturgeon">white sturgeon</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>All pinnipeds are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carnivorous" title="Carnivorous">carnivorous</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Predatory" title="Predatory">predatory</a>. As a whole, they mostly feed on fish and <a href="/wiki/Cephalopod" title="Cephalopod">cephalopods</a>, followed by <a href="/wiki/Crustacean" title="Crustacean">crustaceans</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bivalve" title="Bivalve">bivalves</a>, and then <a href="/wiki/Zooplankton" title="Zooplankton">zooplankton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Endotherm" title="Endotherm">endothermic</a> ("warm-blooded") prey like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_bird" title="Sea bird">sea birds</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[94]</a></sup> While most species are <a href="/wiki/Generalist_and_specialist_species" title="Generalist and specialist species">generalist</a> and opportunistic feeders, a few are specialists.
